Soon after, he left the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Hagan_156-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Hagan-156">[154]</a></sup> Nkrumah was also a very flamboyant leader. _[The New York Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_New_York_Times "The New York Times")_ in 1972 wrote: "During his high‐flying days as the leader of Ghana in the 1950s and early 1960s, Kwame Nkrumah was a flamboyant spellbinder.<sup id="cite_ref-157"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup> At home, he created a [cult of personality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cult_of_personality "Cult of personality") and gloried in the title of _Osagyefo_ ([Akan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akan_language "Akan language") for 'Redeemer'). Abroad, he met with the world's leaders as the first man to lead an African colony to independence after World War II."<sup id="cite_ref-158"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup>

During his tenure as Prime Minister and then first President, Nkrumah succeeded in reducing the political importance of the local chieftaincy (e.g., the [Akan chiefs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akan_Chieftaincy "Akan Chieftaincy") and the [Asantehene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asantehene "Asantehene")).<sup id="cite_ref-159"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup> These chiefs had maintained authority during colonial rule through collaboration with the British authorities; in fact, they were sometimes favored over the local intelligentsia, who made trouble for the British with organizations like the [Aborigines' Rights Protection Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aborigines%27_Rights_Protection_Society "Aborigines' Rights Protection Society").<sup id="cite_ref-160"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup> The Convention People's Party had a strained relationship with the chiefs when it came to power, and this relationship became more hostile as the CPP incited political opposition chiefs and criticized the institution as undemocratic. Acts passed in 1958 and 1959 gave the government more power to dis-stool chiefs directly, and proclaimed government of stool land – and revenues.<sup id="cite_ref-161"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-161">[159]</a></sup> These policies alienated the chiefs and led them to looking favorably on the overthrow of Nkrumah and his Party.<sup id="cite_ref-162"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup>

#### Increased power of the Convention People's Party\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Increased power of the Convention People's Party")\]

In 1962, three younger members of the CPP were brought up on charges of taking part in a plot to blow up Nkrumah's car in a motorcade. The sole evidence against the alleged plotters was that they rode in cars well behind Nkrumah's car.<sup id="cite_ref-BIRTH_OF_THE_CPP_103-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-BIRTH_OF_THE_CPP-103">[101]</a></sup> When the defendants were acquitted, Nkrumah sacked the chief judge of the state security court, then got the CPP-dominated parliament to pass a law allowing a [new trial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double_jeopardy "Double jeopardy").<sup id="cite_ref-163"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup> At this second trial, all three men were convicted and sentenced to death, though these sentences were subsequently commuted to life imprisonment. Shortly afterward, the constitution was amended to give the president the power to summarily remove judges at all levels.<sup id="cite_ref-164"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup>

In 1964, Nkrumah proposed a [constitutional amendment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1964_Ghanaian_constitutional_referendum "1964 Ghanaian constitutional referendum") that would make the CPP [the only legal party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/One-party_state "One-party state"), with Nkrumah as [president for life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_for_life "President for life") of both nation and party. The amendment passed with 99.91 percent of the vote<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="The source given has no mention of the percentage that voted for the amendment, it only makes the opinionated statement that the vote for it was obviously rigged, with no reasoning provided (January 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, an implausibly high total that led observers to condemn the vote as "obviously rigged".<sup id="cite_ref-165"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup> Ghana had effectively been a one-party state since independence. The amendment transformed Nkrumah's presidency into a _de facto_ legal dictatorship.

### Civil service\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Civil service")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Dolazak_predsednika_Gane_Kvame_Nkrumaha_na_konferenciju_nesvrstanih%2C_1961._god.jpg/220px-Dolazak_predsednika_Gane_Kvame_Nkrumaha_na_konferenciju_nesvrstanih%2C_1961._god.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Dolazak_predsednika_Gane_Kvame_Nkrumaha_na_konferenciju_nesvrstanih,_1961._god.jpg)

Arrival of the president of Ghana, Kwame Nkrumah, and president of [Yugoslavia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia "Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia"), [Josip Broz Tito](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito "Josip Broz Tito"), to the [first conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1st_Summit_of_the_Non-Aligned_Movement "1st Summit of the Non-Aligned Movement") of the [Non-Aligned Movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement "Non-Aligned Movement"), [Belgrade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belgrade "Belgrade"), 1961.

After substantial Africanization of the [civil service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghana_Civil_Service "Ghana Civil Service") in 1952–60, the number of [expatriates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expatriate "Expatriate") rose again from 1960 to 1965. Many of the new outside workers came not from the United Kingdom but from the Soviet Union, Poland, Czechoslovakia, [Yugoslavia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia "Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia"), and Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-Ayee_166-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Ayee-166">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stanek_2020_6-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Stanek_2020-6">[6]</a></sup>

### Education\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Education")\]

In 1951, the CPP created the Accelerated Development Plan for Education. This plan set up a six-year primary course, to be attended as close to universally as possible, with a range of possibilities to follow.<sup id="cite_ref-167"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-167">[165]</a></sup> All children were to learn arithmetic, as well as gain "a sound foundation for citizenship with permanent literacy in both English and the vernacular." Primary education became [compulsory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compulsory_education "Compulsory education") in 1962. The plan also stated that religious schools would no longer receive funding, and that some existing missionary schools would be taken over by government.<sup id="cite_ref-Haizel_168-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Haizel-168">[166]</a></sup>

> We in Ghana, are committed to the building of an industrialised socialist society.  We cannot afford to sit still and be mere passive onlookers.  We must ourselves take part in the pursuit of scientific and technological research as a means of providing the basis for our socialist society, Socialism without science is void.…
> 
> We need also to reach out to the mass of the people who have not had the opportunities of formal education.  We must use every means of mass communication – the press, the radio, television and films – to carry science to the whole population – to the people. ...  
> It is most important that our people should not only be instructed in science but that they should take part in it, apply it themselves in their own ways.  For science is not just a subject to be learned out of a book or from a teacher.   It is a way of life, a way of tackling any problem which one can only master by using it for oneself.  We must have science clubs in which our people can develop their own talents for discovery and invention.

— Kwame Nkrumah "Speech delivered by Osagyefo the President at the Laying of the Foundation Stone of Ghana's Atomic Reactor at Kwabenya on 25th November, 1964"<sup id="cite_ref-Haizel_168-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Haizel-168">[166]</a></sup>

In 1961, Nkrumah laid the first stones in the foundation of the [Kwame Nkrumah Ideological Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah_Ideological_Institute "Kwame Nkrumah Ideological Institute") created to train Ghanaian civil servants as well as promote Pan-Africanism. In 1964, all students entering college in Ghana were required to attend a two-week "[ideological orientation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indoctrination "Indoctrination")" at the institute.<sup id="cite_ref-NRCReport_169-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-NRCReport-169">[167]</a></sup> Nkrumah remarked that "trainees should be made to realize the party's ideology is religion, and should be practiced faithfully and fervently."<sup id="cite_ref-170"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup>

In 1964, Nkrumah brought forth the Seven Year Development Plan for National Reconstruction and Development, which identified education as a key source of development and called for the expansion of secondary technical schools.<sup id="cite_ref-171"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup> Secondary education would also include "in-service training programmes". As Nkrumah told Parliament: "Employers, both public and private, will be expected to make a far greater contribution to labour training through individual factory and farm schools, industry-wide training schemes, day release, payment for attendance at short courses and evening classes." This training would be indirectly subsidized with tax credits and import allocations.<sup id="cite_ref-172"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-172">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haizel_168-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Haizel-168">[166]</a></sup>

In 1952, the Artisan Trading Scheme, arranged with the Colonial Office and UK Ministry of Labour, provided for a few experts in every field to travel to Britain for technical education. Kumasi Technical Institute was founded in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-173"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-173">[171]</a></sup> In September 1960, it added the Technical Teacher Training Centre. In 1961, the CPP passed the Apprentice Act, which created a general Apprenticeship Board along with committees for each industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Haizel_168-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Haizel-168">[166]</a></sup>

### Culture\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Culture")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/Pahor_Labib_Watani_0.10_LRcrop1_%282%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pahor_Labib_Watani_0.10_LRcrop1_(2).jpg)

Nkrumah with Egyptian [Egyptologist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egyptology "Egyptology") [Pahor Labib](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pahor_Labib "Pahor Labib") at the [Coptic Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coptic_Museum "Coptic Museum"), 1956

Nkrumah was an ardent promoter of [pan-Africanism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-Africanism "Pan-Africanism"), seeing the movement as the "quest for regional integration of the whole of the African continent". The period of Nkrumah's active political involvement has been described as the "golden age of high pan-African ambitions"; the continent had experienced [rising nationalist movements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_nationalism "African nationalism") and [decolonization by most European colonial powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decolonisation_of_Africa "Decolonisation of Africa"), and historians have noted that "the narrative of rebirth and solidarity had gained momentum within the pan-Africanist movement". Reflecting his African heritage, Nkrumah frequently eschewed [Western fashion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_dress_codes "Western dress codes"), donning a [fugu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghanaian_smock "Ghanaian smock") (a Northern attire) made with Southern-produced [Kente cloth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kente_cloth "Kente cloth"), a symbol of his identity as a representative of the entire country.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.fr_174-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-books.google.fr-174">[172]</a></sup> He oversaw the opening of the [Ghana Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Museum_of_Ghana "National Museum of Ghana") on 5 March 1957; the Arts Council of Ghana, a wing of the Ministry of Education and Culture, in 1958; the Research Library on African Affairs in June 1961; and the Ghana Film Corporation in 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-Hagan_156-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Hagan-156">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-175">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Legum,_C._1965_p._41_176-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Legum,_C._1965_p._41-176">[174]</a></sup> In 1962, Nkrumah opened the [Institute of African Studies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Institute_of_African_Studies "Institute of African Studies").<sup id="cite_ref-Haizel_168-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Haizel-168">[166]</a></sup>

A campaign against nudity in the northern part of the country received special attention from Nkrumah, who reportedly deployed Propaganda Secretary [Hannah Cudjoe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hannah_Kudjoe "Hannah Kudjoe") to respond. Cudjoe also formed the [Ghana Women's League](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ghana_Women%27s_League&action=edit&redlink=1 "Ghana Women's League (page does not exist)"), which advanced the Party's agenda on nutrition, raising children, and wearing clothing.<sup id="cite_ref-177"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-177">[175]</a></sup> The League also led a demonstration against the [detonation of French nuclear weapons in the Sahara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/France_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction#Saharan_experiments_centers_(1960%E2%80%9366) "France and weapons of mass destruction").<sup id="cite_ref-Manuh_178-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Manuh-178">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Allman_179-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Allman-179">[177]</a></sup> Cudjoe was eventually demoted with the consolidation of national women's groups, and marginalized within the Party structure.<sup id="cite_ref-Allman_179-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Allman-179">[177]</a></sup>

Laws passed in 1959 and 1960 designated special positions in parliament to be held by [women](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Women_in_Ghana "Women in Ghana"). Some women were promoted to the CPP Central Committee. Women attended more universities, took up more professions including medicine and law, and went on professional trips to Israel, the Soviet Union, and the Eastern Bloc. Women also entered the army and air force. Most women remained in agriculture and trade; some received assistance from the [Co-operative Movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Co-operative_Movement "Co-operative Movement").<sup id="cite_ref-180"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-180">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hagan2_65-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Hagan2-65">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Manuh_178-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Manuh-178">[176]</a></sup>

Nkrumah's image was widely disseminated, for example, on postage stamps and on money, in the style of monarchs – providing fodder for accusations of a [Nkrumahist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nkrumaism "Nkrumaism") personality cult.<sup id="cite_ref-181"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup>

### Media\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Media")\]

In 1957, Nkrumah created a well-funded [Ghana News Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghana_News_Agency "Ghana News Agency") to generate domestic news and disseminate it abroad. In ten years time the GNA had 8045 km of domestic telegraph line, and maintained stations in Lagos, Nairobi, London and New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-182"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ansah_183-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Ansah-183">[181]</a></sup>

> To the true African journalist, his newspaper is a collective organiser, a collective instrument of mobilisation and a collective educator—a weapon, first and foremost, to overthrow colonialism and imperialism and to assist total African independence and unity.

— Kwame Nkrumah at the Second Conference of African Journalists; Accra, November 11, 1963<sup id="cite_ref-Ansah_183-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Ansah-183">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-184">[182]</a></sup>

Nkrumah consolidated state control over newspapers, establishing the _[Ghanaian Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghanaian_Times "Ghanaian Times")_ in 1958 and then in 1962 obtaining its competitor, the _[Daily Graphic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daily_Graphic_(Ghana) "Daily Graphic (Ghana)")_, from the [Mirror Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mirror_Group "Mirror Group") of London.<sup id="cite_ref-185"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup> As he wrote in _Africa Must Unite_: "It is part of our revolutionary credo that within the competitive system of capitalism, the press cannot function in accordance with a strict regard for the sacredness of facts, and that the press, therefore, should not remain in private hands." Starting in 1960, he invoked the right of [pre-publication censorship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pre-publication_censorship "Pre-publication censorship") of all news.<sup id="cite_ref-Ansah_183-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Ansah-183">[181]</a></sup>

The [Gold Coast Broadcasting Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_Coast_Broadcasting_Service "Gold Coast Broadcasting Service") was established in 1954 and revamped as the [Ghana Broadcasting Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghana_Broadcasting_Corporation "Ghana Broadcasting Corporation") (GBC). Many television broadcasts featured Nkrumah, commenting for example on the problematic "insolence and laziness of boys and girls".<sup id="cite_ref-186"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup> Before celebrations of May Day, 1963, Nkrumah went on television to announce the expansion of Ghana's [Young Pioneers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Young_Pioneer "Young Pioneer"), the introduction of a National Pledge, the beginning of a National Flag salute in schools, and the creation of a National Training program to inculcate virtue and the spirit of service among Ghanaian youth.<sup id="cite_ref-187"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-187">[185]</a></sup> Nkrumah outlined his views on the role of Ghanaian television to Parliament on 15 October 1963 saying, "Ghana's television will not cater for cheap entertainment or commercialism; its paramount objective will be education in its broadest and purest sense."<sup id="cite_ref-188"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-188">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haizel_168-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Haizel-168">[166]</a></sup>

As per the 1965 Instrument of Incorporation of the Ghana Broadcasting Corporation, the Minister of Information and Broadcasting had "powers of direction" over the media, and the President had the power "at any time, if he is satisfied that it is in the national interest to do so, take over the control and management of the affairs or any part of the functions of the Corporation," hiring, firing, reorganizing, and making other commands at will.<sup id="cite_ref-189"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-189">[187]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ansah_183-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Ansah-183">[181]</a></sup>

Radio programmes, designed in part to reach non-reading members of the public, were a major focus of the Ghana Broadcasting Corporation. In 1961, the GBC formed an [external service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_broadcasting "International broadcasting") broadcasting in English, French, Arabic, Swahili, Portuguese and [Hausa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hausa_language "Hausa language").<sup id="cite_ref-Palgrave_Macmillan_190-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Palgrave_Macmillan-190">[188]</a></sup> Using four 100-kilowatt transmitters and two 250-kilowatt transmitters, the GBC External Service broadcast 110 hours of Pan-Africanist programming to Africa and Europe each week.<sup id="cite_ref-Ansah_183-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Ansah-183">[181]</a></sup>

He refused advertising in all media, beginning with the _Evening News_ of 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-Ansah_183-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Ansah-183">[181]</a></sup>

### Economic policy\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Economic policy")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Akosombo_Dam_hydroelectric_plant.jpg/220px-Akosombo_Dam_hydroelectric_plant.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Akosombo_Dam_hydroelectric_plant.jpg)

[Akosombo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akosombo_Dam "Akosombo Dam") [hydroelectric dam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydroelectric_dam "Hydroelectric dam")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/ASC_Leiden_-_NSAG_-_van_Es_17-016_-_Visiting_President_Kwame_Nkrumah_is_waving_with_a_tropical_hat_-_Volta_Dam%2C_Akosombo%2C_Ghana_-_late_February_1962.tif/lossy-page1-220px-ASC_Leiden_-_NSAG_-_van_Es_17-016_-_Visiting_President_Kwame_Nkrumah_is_waving_with_a_tropical_hat_-_Volta_Dam%2C_Akosombo%2C_Ghana_-_late_February_1962.tif.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ASC_Leiden_-_NSAG_-_van_Es_17-016_-_Visiting_President_Kwame_Nkrumah_is_waving_with_a_tropical_hat_-_Volta_Dam,_Akosombo,_Ghana_-_late_February_1962.tif)

Nkrumah visits the Akosombo Dam under construction with his [1961 Chevrolet Impala](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevrolet_Impala "Chevrolet Impala") presidential car, February 1962.

The Gold Coast had been among the wealthiest and most socially advanced areas in Africa, with schools, railways, hospitals, social security, and an advanced economy.<sup id="cite_ref-191"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup>

Nkrumah attempted to rapidly [industrialize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Industrialization "Industrialization") Ghana's economy. He reasoned that if Ghana escaped the colonial trade system by reducing dependence on foreign [capital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capital_(economics) "Capital (economics)"), technology, and material goods, it could become truly independent.<sup id="cite_ref-192"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-192">[190]</a></sup>

After the Ten Year Development Plan, Nkrumah brought forth the Second Development Plan in 1959. This plan called for the development of manufacturing: 600 factories producing 100 varieties of product.<sup id="cite_ref-Darko_193-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Darko-193">[191]</a></sup>

The Statutory Corporations Act, passed in November 1959 and revised in 1961 and 1964, created the legal framework for public corporations, which included state enterprises. This law placed the country's major corporations under the direction of government ministers. The State Enterprises Secretariat office was located in [Flagstaff House](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Flagstaff_House "The Flagstaff House") and under the direct control of the president.<sup id="cite_ref-194"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Asante_195-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Asante-195">[193]</a></sup>

After visiting the Soviet Union, Eastern Europe and China in 1961, Nkrumah apparently became still more convinced of the need for state control of the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-196"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-196">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Asante_195-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Asante-195">[193]</a></sup>

Nkrumah's time in office began successfully: forestry, fishing, and cattle-breeding expanded, production of cocoa (Ghana's main export) doubled, and modest deposits of bauxite and gold were exploited more effectively.<sup id="cite_ref-197"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-197">[195]</a></sup> The construction of a dam on the River Volta (launched in 1961) provided water for irrigation and hydro-electric power, which produced enough electricity for the towns and for a new aluminum plant. Government funds were also provided for village projects in which local people built schools and roads,<sup id="cite_ref-198"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-199">[197]</a></sup> while free health care and education were introduced.<sup id="cite_ref-200"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-201">[199]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-202">[200]</a></sup>

A Seven-Year Plan introduced in 1964 focused on further industrialization, emphasizing domestic substitutes for common imports, modernization of the building materials industry, machine making, [electrification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrification "Electrification") and electronics.<sup id="cite_ref-203"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Darko_193-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Darko-193">[191]</a></sup>

#### Energy projects\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Energy projects")\]

Nkrumah's advocacy of industrial development, with help of longtime friend and Minister of Finance, [Komla Agbeli Gbedema](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Komla_Agbeli_Gbedema "Komla Agbeli Gbedema"), led to the Volta River Project: the construction of a hydroelectric power plant, the [Akosombo Dam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akosombo_Dam "Akosombo Dam") on the [Volta River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volta_River "Volta River") in eastern Ghana.<sup id="cite_ref-204"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-204">[202]</a></sup> The Volta River Project was the centrepiece of Nkrumah's economic programme. On 20 February 1958, he told the National Assembly: "It is my strong belief that the Volta River Project provides the quickest and most certain method of leading us towards economic independence."<sup id="cite_ref-205"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-205">[203]</a></sup> Ghana used assistance from the United States, Israel and the World Bank in constructing the dam.<sup id="cite_ref-206"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Asamoah_207-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Asamoah-207">[205]</a></sup>

[Kaiser Aluminum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaiser_Aluminum "Kaiser Aluminum") agreed to build the dam for Nkrumah, but restricted what could be produced using the power generated. Nkrumah borrowed money to build the dam, and placed Ghana in debt. To finance the debt, he raised taxes on the cocoa farmers in the south. This accentuated regional differences and jealousy. The dam was completed and opened by Nkrumah amidst global publicity on 22 January 1966.

Nkrumah initiated the Ghana [Nuclear Reactor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_reactor "Nuclear reactor") Project in 1961, created the [Ghana Atomic Energy Commission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghana_Atomic_Energy_Commission "Ghana Atomic Energy Commission") in 1963, and in 1964 laid the first stone in the building of an atomic energy facility.<sup id="cite_ref-208"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-208">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FoundationStone_209-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-FoundationStone-209">[207]</a></sup>

#### Cocoa\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Cocoa")\]

In 1954 the world price of cocoa rose from £150 to £450 per ton. Rather than allowing cocoa farmers to keep the windfall, Nkrumah appropriated the increased revenue via central government levies, then invested the capital into various national development projects.<sup id="cite_ref-210"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup> This policy alienated one of the major constituencies that helped him come to power.<sup id="cite_ref-211"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-211">[209]</a></sup>

Prices continued to fluctuate. In 1960 one ton of cocoa sold for £250 in London. By August 1965 this price had dropped to £91, one fifth of its value ten years before.<sup id="cite_ref-Gebe_75-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Gebe-75">[74]</a></sup> The quick price decline caused the government's reliance on the reserves and forced farmers to take a portion of their earning in bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-212"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup>

### Foreign and military policy\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Foreign and military policy")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Nkrumah%2C_his_family_and_Nasser%2C_1965.jpg/309px-Nkrumah%2C_his_family_and_Nasser%2C_1965.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nkrumah,_his_family_and_Nasser,_1965.jpg)

Nkrumah and his family meeting Egyptian President [Gamal Abdel Nasser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser "Gamal Abdel Nasser") during the 1965 [Organization of African Unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity "Organisation of African Unity") Summit in Accra.

Nkrumah actively promoted a policy of Pan-Africanism from the beginning of his presidency. This entailed the creation of a series of new international organizations, which held their inaugural meetings in Accra.<sup id="cite_ref-213"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-213">[211]</a></sup> These were:

-   the First Conference of Independent States, in April 1958;<sup id="cite_ref-214"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup>
-   the more inclusive [All-African Peoples' Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/All-African_Peoples%27_Conference "All-African Peoples' Conference"), with representatives from 62 nationalist organizations from across the continent, in December 1958;<sup id="cite_ref-215"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-215">[213]</a></sup>
-   the All-African Trade Union Federation, meeting in November 1959, to coordinate the African labour movement;<sup id="cite_ref-216"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-216">[214]</a></sup>
-   the Positive Action and Security in Africa conference, in April 1960, discussing Algeria, South Africa, and French [nuclear weapons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_weapon "Nuclear weapon") testing;<sup id="cite_ref-217"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-217">[215]</a></sup>
-   the Conference of African Women, on 18 July 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-Gebe_75-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Gebe-75">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Asamoah_207-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Asamoah-207">[205]</a></sup>

Meanwhile, Ghana withdrew from colonial organizations including [West Africa Airways Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=West_Africa_Airways_Corporation&action=edit&redlink=1 "West Africa Airways Corporation (page does not exist)"), the [West African Currency Board](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_African_Currency_Board "West African Currency Board"), the [West African Cocoa Research Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=West_African_Cocoa_Research_Institute&action=edit&redlink=1 "West African Cocoa Research Institute (page does not exist)"), and the [West African Court of Appeal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_African_Court_of_Appeal "West African Court of Appeal").<sup id="cite_ref-218"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-218">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gebe_75-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Gebe-75">[74]</a></sup>

In the [Year of Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Year_of_Africa "Year of Africa"), 1960, Nkrumah negotiated the creation of a [Union of African States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Union_of_African_States "Union of African States"), a political alliance between Ghana, Guinea, and Mali. Immediately, they formed a women's group called Women of the Union of African States.<sup id="cite_ref-219"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-219">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Manuh_178-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Manuh-178">[176]</a></sup>

Nkrumah was a leading figure in the short-lived [Casablanca Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Casablanca_Group "Casablanca Group") of African leaders, which sought to achieve pan-African unity and harmony through deep political, economic, and military integration of the continent in the early 1960s prior to the establishment of the [Organization of African Unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity "Organisation of African Unity") (OAU).<sup id="cite_ref-220"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup> In 1961, he was a participant in the [1st Summit of the Non-Aligned Movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1st_Summit_of_the_Non-Aligned_Movement "1st Summit of the Non-Aligned Movement") in [Belgrade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belgrade "Belgrade"), [FPR Yugoslavia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FPR_Yugoslavia "FPR Yugoslavia") making Ghana one of the founding members of the [Non-Aligned Movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement "Non-Aligned Movement").

Nkrumah was instrumental in the creation of the OAU in [Addis Ababa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Addis_Ababa "Addis Ababa") in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-Hagan_156-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Hagan-156">[154]</a></sup> He aspired to create a united military force, the African High Command, which Ghana would substantially lead, and committed to this vision in Article 2 of [the 1960 Republican Constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1960_Ghanaian_constitutional_referendum "1960 Ghanaian constitutional referendum"):<sup id="cite_ref-222"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup>"In the confident expectation of an early surrender of sovereignty to a union of African states and territories, the people now confer on Parliament the power to provide for the surrender of the whole or any part of the sovereignty of Ghana."<sup id="cite_ref-Gebe_75-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Gebe-75">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup>

He was also a proponent of the United Nations, but critical of the Great Powers' ability to control it.<sup id="cite_ref-Asamoah_207-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Asamoah-207">[205]</a></sup>

Nkrumah opposed the entry of African states into the Common Market of the [European Economic Community](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Economic_Community "European Economic Community"), a status given to many former French colonies and considered by Nigeria. Instead, Nkrumah advocated, in a speech given on 7 April 1960,<sup id="cite_ref-224"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-224">[222]</a></sup>

> an African common market, a common currency area and the development of communications of all kinds to allow the free flow of goods and services. International capital can be attracted to such viable economic areas, but it would not be attracted to a divided and balkanized Africa, with each small region engaged in senseless and suicidal economic competition with its neighbours.<sup id="cite_ref-225"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-225">[223]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Asamoah_207-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Asamoah-207">[205]</a></sup>

Nkrumah sought to exploit the Cold War rivalry between the United States and the Soviet Union in order to gain maximum concessions from both sides in their geopolitical attempts to outmanoeuvre one another in West Africa and elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-226"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup> This was exemplified by the Volta River Dam Project and its back-and-forth oscillation between Soviet and Western financial backing.<sup id="cite_ref-227"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-227">[225]</a></sup>

#### Armed forces\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Armed forces")\]

In 1956, the Gold Coast took control of the [Royal West African Frontier Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_West_African_Frontier_Force "Royal West African Frontier Force") (RWAFF), [Gold Coast Regiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_Coast_Regiment "Gold Coast Regiment"), from the British War Office. This force had formerly been deployed to quell internal dissent, and occasionally to fight in wars: most recently, in World War II, against the Japanese in India and Burma.<sup id="cite_ref-228"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-228">[226]</a></sup> The most senior officers in this force were British, and, although training of African officers began in 1947, only 28 of 212 officers in December 1956 were indigenous Africans. The British officers still received British salaries, which vastly exceeded those allotted to their Ghanaian counterparts. Concerned about a possible military coup, Nkrumah delayed the placement of African officers in top leadership roles.<sup id="cite_ref-Hutchful_229-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Hutchful-229">[227]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup>

Nkrumah quickly established the [Ghanaian Air Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghanaian_Air_Force "Ghanaian Air Force"), acquiring 14 _[Beaver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_Havilland_Canada_DHC-2_Beaver "De Havilland Canada DHC-2 Beaver")_ airplanes from Canada and setting up a flight school with British instructors. _[Otters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_Havilland_Canada_DHC-3_Otter "De Havilland Canada DHC-3 Otter")_, _[Caribou](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_Havilland_Canada_DHC-4_Caribou "De Havilland Canada DHC-4 Caribou"),_ and _[Chipmunks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_Havilland_Canada_DHC-1_Chipmunk "De Havilland Canada DHC-1 Chipmunk")_ were to follow.<sup id="cite_ref-Hutchful_229-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Hutchful-229">[227]</a></sup> Ghana also obtained four [Ilyushin-18](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ilyushin_Il-18 "Ilyushin Il-18") aircraft from the Soviet Union. Preparation began in April 1959 with assistance from India and Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-231"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup> Nkrumah also established a gliding school led by [Hanna Reitsch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanna_Reitsch "Hanna Reitsch") and [J.E.S. de Graft-Hayford](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J.E.S._de_Graft-Hayford "J.E.S. de Graft-Hayford").

The [Ghanaian Navy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghanaian_Navy "Ghanaian Navy") received two inshore minesweepers with 40mm and 20mm guns, the _Afadzato_ and the _Yogaga_, from Britain in December 1959. It subsequently received the _Elmina_ and the _Komenda_, seaward defence boats with 40-millimetre guns.<sup id="cite_ref-Hutchful_229-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Hutchful-229">[227]</a></sup> The Navy's flagship, and training ship, was the _Achimota_, a British yacht constructed during World War II. In 1961, the Navy ordered two 600-ton corvettes, the _Keta_ and _Kromantse,_ from [Vosper & Company](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vospers "Vospers") and received them in 1967. It also procured four Soviet patrol boats. Naval officers were trained at the [Britannia Royal Naval College](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Britannia_Royal_Naval_College "Britannia Royal Naval College") in Dartmouth.<sup id="cite_ref-232"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-232">[230]</a></sup> The Ghanaian military budget rose each year, from $9.35 million (US dollars) in 1958 to $47 million in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-233"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-233">[231]</a></sup>

The first international deployment of the Ghanaian armed forces was to the [Congo (Léopoldville/Kinshasa)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo "Democratic Republic of the Congo"), where Ghanaian troops were airlifted in 1960 at the beginning of the [Congo Crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Congo_Crisis "Congo Crisis").<sup id="cite_ref-Hutchful_229-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Hutchful-229">[227]</a></sup> One week after Belgian troops occupied the lucrative mining province of [Katanga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Katanga_Province "Katanga Province"), Ghana dispatched more than a thousand of its own troops to join a United Nations force.<sup id="cite_ref-Beynahm93_234-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Beynahm93-234">[232]</a></sup> The use of British officers in this context was politically unacceptable, and this event occasioned a hasty transfer of officer positions to Ghanaians.<sup id="cite_ref-Hutchful_229-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Hutchful-229">[227]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-235">[233]</a></sup> The Congo war was long and difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-Beynahm93_234-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Beynahm93-234">[232]</a></sup> On 19 January 1961 the Third Infantry Battalion mutinied. On 28 April 1961, 43 men [were massacred in a surprise attack](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Port_Francqui_incident "Port Francqui incident") by the Congolese army.<sup id="cite_ref-236"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-236">[234]</a></sup>

Ghana also gave military support to rebels fighting against [Ian Smith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ian_Smith "Ian Smith")'s [white-minority government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minority_rule "Minority rule") in [Rhodesia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhodesia "Rhodesia") (now [Zimbabwe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zimbabwe "Zimbabwe")), which had unilaterally declared independence from Britain in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-237"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-237">[235]</a></sup>

#### Relationship with Communist world\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Relationship with Communist world")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Che_Guevara_Nkrumah_1965-01_Ghana.jpg/220px-Che_Guevara_Nkrumah_1965-01_Ghana.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Che_Guevara_Nkrumah_1965-01_Ghana.jpg)

Nkrumah with [Ernesto "Che" Guevara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara "Che Guevara"), January 1965

In 1961, Nkrumah went on tour through Eastern Europe, proclaiming solidarity with the Soviet Union and the People's Republic of China.<sup id="cite_ref-Gebe_75-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Gebe-75">[74]</a></sup> Nkrumah's clothing changed to the Chinese-supplied [Mao suit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mao_suit "Mao suit").<sup id="cite_ref-238"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-238">[236]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-239">[237]</a></sup>

In 1962 Kwame Nkrumah was awarded the [Lenin Peace Prize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lenin_Peace_Prize "Lenin Peace Prize") by the [Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union").<sup id="cite_ref-240"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-240">[238]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Ghana_3.jpg/220px-Ghana_3.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ghana_3.jpg)

Anti-Nkrumah placard at a demonstration after the coup.

## 1966 coup d'état\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: 1966 coup d'état")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana%2C_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_%28JFKWHP-AR6409-B%29.jpg/221px-President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana%2C_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_%28JFKWHP-AR6409-B%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana,_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_(JFKWHP-AR6409-B).jpg)

Kwame Nkrumah with U.S. President John F. Kennedy, 8 March 1961

In February 1966, while Nkrumah was on a state visit to [North Vietnam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Vietnam "North Vietnam") and China, his government was overthrown in a violent _coup d'état_ led by the national [military](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghana_Armed_Forces "Ghana Armed Forces") and [police](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghana_Police_Service "Ghana Police Service") forces, with backing from the [civil service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghana_Civil_Service "Ghana Civil Service").<sup id="cite_ref-241"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-241">[239]</a></sup> The conspirators, led by [Joseph Arthur Ankrah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Arthur_Ankrah "Joseph Arthur Ankrah"), named themselves the [National Liberation Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Liberation_Council "National Liberation Council") and ruled as a military government for three years. Nkrumah did not learn of the coup until he arrived in China. After the coup, Nkrumah stayed in Beijing for four days, and Premier [Zhou Enlai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zhou_Enlai "Zhou Enlai") treated him with courtesy.<sup id="cite_ref-242"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-242">[240]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-243"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-243">[241]</a></sup>

Nkrumah alluded to American involvement in the coup in his 1969 memoir, _Dark Days in Ghana_; he may have based this conclusion on documents shown to him by the [KGB](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/KGB "KGB").<sup id="cite_ref-244"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-244">[242]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-245">[243]</a></sup> In 1978 [John Stockwell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Stockwell_(CIA_officer) "John Stockwell (CIA officer)"), former Chief of the Angola Task Force of the [CIA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency "Central Intelligence Agency") turned critic, wrote that agents at the CIA's Accra station "maintained intimate contact with the plotters as a coup was hatched". Afterward, "inside CIA headquarters the Accra station was given full, if unofficial credit for the eventual coup. ...None of this was adequately reflected in the agency's written records."<sup id="cite_ref-246"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-246">[244]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-247">[245]</a></sup> Later that same year, [Seymour Hersh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seymour_Hersh "Seymour Hersh"), then at _[The New York Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_New_York_Times "The New York Times")_, defended Stockwell's account, citing "first hand intelligence sources". He claimed that "many CIA operatives in Africa considered the agency's role in the overthrow of Dr. Nkrumah to have been pivotal."<sup id="cite_ref-248"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-248">[246]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-249">[247]</a></sup> These claims have never been verified.<sup id="cite_ref-250"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-250">[248]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-251">[249]</a></sup>

Following the coup, Ghana realigned itself internationally, cutting its close ties to Guinea and the Eastern Bloc, accepting a new friendship with the [Western Bloc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Bloc "Western Bloc"), and inviting the [International Monetary Fund](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund "International Monetary Fund") and [World Bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank") to take a leading role in managing the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-252"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup><sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (August 2022)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> With this reversal, accentuated by the expulsion of immigrants and a new willingness to negotiate with [apartheid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apartheid "Apartheid") South Africa, Ghana lost a good deal of its stature in the eyes of African nationalists.<sup id="cite_ref-253"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gebe_75-6"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Gebe-75">[74]</a></sup>

In assessing Nkrumah's legacy, [Edward Luttwak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Luttwak "Edward Luttwak") argued that he was undone by the growth of [political consciousness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_consciousness "Political consciousness") and his inability to repress potential opponents:

> Nkrumah, in spite of his eccentricities, was largely defeated by his own success: the by-product of the considerable economic development achieved by Ghana was to stimulate and educate the masses and the new elite; their attitude to Nkrumah's regime became more and more critical in the light of the education the regime itself provided. When this happens, more and more repression and propaganda are needed to maintain political stability. In spite of considerable efforts, Nkrumah was unable to build a sufficiently ruthless police system. The cause of his downfall was not, therefore, the mismanagement of the economy—which was considerable—but rather the success of much of the development effort.

## Exile and death\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Exile and death")\]

Nkrumah died on April 27, 1972, in [Bucharest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bucharest "Bucharest"), the capital of [Romania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_Romania "Socialist Republic of Romania").<sup id="cite_ref-:02_254-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-:02-254">[252]</a></sup> Nkrumah died of an unknown but apparently incurable sickness. Since the coup, he had since that time been living in the Guinean capital of Conakry laying low.

## Tributes and Legacy\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Tributes and Legacy")\]

Over his lifetime, Nkrumah was awarded honorary doctorates by many universities including [Lincoln University (Pennsylvania)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lincoln_University_(Pennsylvania) "Lincoln University (Pennsylvania)"), [Moscow State University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moscow_State_University "Moscow State University") (USSR), [Cairo University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cairo_University "Cairo University") ([Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egypt "Egypt")), [Jagiellonian University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jagiellonian_University "Jagiellonian University") ([Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poland "Poland")), and [Humboldt University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humboldt_University "Humboldt University") ([East Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Germany "East Germany")).<sup id="cite_ref-Education_For_Leadership_255-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Education_For_Leadership-255">[253]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Kwame_Nkrumah_Memorial_Park.jpg/220px-Kwame_Nkrumah_Memorial_Park.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kwame_Nkrumah_Memorial_Park.jpg)

Kwame Nkrumah Memorial Park and Mausoleum

In 2000, he was voted African Man of the Millennium by listeners to the [BBC World Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBC_World_Service "BBC World Service"), being described by the BBC as a "Hero of Independence", and an "International symbol of freedom as the leader of the first black African country to shake off the chains of colonial rule."<sup id="cite_ref-256"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Kwame_nkrumah_grave_accra_ghana.jpg/215px-Kwame_nkrumah_grave_accra_ghana.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kwame_nkrumah_grave_accra_ghana.jpg)

According to intelligence documents released by the [U.S. Department of State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Department_of_State "U.S. Department of State")'s [Office of the Historian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Office_of_the_Historian "Office of the Historian"), "Nkrumah was doing more to undermine \[U.S. government\] interests than any other black African."<sup id="cite_ref-258"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-258">[256]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah.jpg/220px-Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah.jpg)

Statue of Kwame Nkrumah in Owerri, [Imo State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imo_State "Imo State"), [Nigeria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nigeria "Nigeria")

In September 2009, President [John Atta Mills](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Atta_Mills "John Atta Mills") declared 21 September (the 100th anniversary of Kwame Nkrumah's birth) to be [Founders' Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Ghana "Public holidays in Ghana"), a statutory holiday in Ghana to celebrate the legacy of Kwame Nkrumah.<sup id="cite_ref-259"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-259">[257]</a></sup> In April 2019, President Akufo-Addo approved the Public Holidays (Amendment) Act 2019 which changed 21 September from Founders' Day to [Kwame Nkrumah Memorial Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Founders%27_Day_(Ghana) "Founders' Day (Ghana)").<sup id="cite_ref-260"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-260">[258]</a></sup>

Nkrumah generally took a [non-aligned](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement "Non-Aligned Movement") Marxist perspective on economics, and believed [capitalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capitalism "Capitalism") had malignant effects that were going to stay with Africa for a long time.<sup id="cite_ref-261"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup> Although he was clear on distancing himself from the [African socialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_socialism "African socialism") of many of his contemporaries, Nkrumah argued that [socialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialism "Socialism") was the system that would best accommodate the changes that capitalism had brought, while still respecting African values.<sup id="cite_ref-262"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-262">[260]</a></sup> He specifically addresses these issues and his politics in a 1967 essay entitled "African Socialism Revisited":

> We know that the traditional African society was founded on principles of egalitarianism. In its actual workings, however, it had various shortcomings. Its humanist impulse, nevertheless, is something that continues to urge us towards our all-African socialist reconstruction. We postulate each man to be an end in himself, not merely a means; and we accept the necessity of guaranteeing each man [equal opportunities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equal_opportunity "Equal opportunity") for his development. The implications of this for sociopolitical practice have to be worked out scientifically, and the necessary social and economic policies pursued with resolution. Any meaningful humanism must begin from egalitarianism and must lead to objectively chosen policies for safeguarding and sustaining [egalitarianism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egalitarianism "Egalitarianism"). Hence, socialism. Hence, also, [scientific socialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_socialism "Scientific socialism").<sup id="cite_ref-263"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-263">[261]</a></sup>

Nkrumah was also best-known politically for his strong commitment to and promotion of [pan-Africanism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-Africanism "Pan-Africanism"). He was inspired by the writings of black intellectuals such as [Marcus Garvey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marcus_Garvey "Marcus Garvey"), [W. E. B. Du Bois](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/W._E._B._Du_Bois "W. E. B. Du Bois"), and [George Padmore](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Padmore "George Padmore"), and his relationships with them. Much of his understanding and relationship to these men was created during his years in America as a student.<sup id="cite_ref-264"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-264">[262]</a></sup> Some would argue that his greatest inspiration was Marcus Garvey,<sup id="cite_ref-265"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-265">[263]</a></sup> although he also had a meaningful relationship with [C. L. R. James](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C._L._R._James "C. L. R. James"). Nkrumah looked to these men to craft a general solution to the ills of Africa. To follow in these intellectual footsteps Nkrumah had intended to continue his education in London, but found himself involved in direct activism.<sup id="cite_ref-266"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-266">[264]</a></sup> Then, motivated by advice from Du Bois, Nkrumah decided to focus on creating peace in Africa. He became a passionate advocate of the "African Personality", embodied in the slogan "Africa for the Africans", earlier popularised by Edward Wilmont Blyden, and he viewed political independence as a prerequisite for economic independence.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin_73-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Martin-73">[72]</a></sup> Nkrumah's dedications to pan-Africanism in action attracted these intellectuals to his Ghanaian projects. Many Americans, such as Du Bois and [Kwame Ture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Ture "Kwame Ture"), moved to Ghana to join him in his efforts. These men<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2021)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> are buried there today.<sup id="cite_ref-267"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-267">[265]</a></sup> His press officer for six years was the Grenadian anticolonialist [Sam Morris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sam_Morris_(activist) "Sam Morris (activist)"). Nkrumah's biggest success in this area was his significant influence in the founding of the [Organisation of African Unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity "Organisation of African Unity").<sup id="cite_ref-268"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-268">[266]</a></sup>

Nkrumah also became a symbol for black liberation in the United States. When in 1958 the [Harlem Lawyers Association](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metropolitan_Black_Bar_Association "Metropolitan Black Bar Association") had an event in Nkrumah's honour, diplomat [Ralph Bunche](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ralph_Bunche "Ralph Bunche") told him:

> We salute you, Kwame Nkrumah, not only because you are Prime Minister of Ghana, although this is cause enough. We salute you because you are a true and living representation of our hopes and ideals, of the determination we have to be accepted fully as equal beings, of the pride we have held and nurtured in our African origin, of the freedom of which we know we are capable, of the freedom in which we believe, of the dignity imperative to our stature as men.<sup id="cite_ref-269"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-269">[267]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Asamoah_207-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Asamoah-207">[205]</a></sup>

In 1961, Nkrumah delivered a speech called "I Speak Of Freedom". During this speech he talked about how "Africa could become one of the greatest forces for good in the world".<sup id="cite_ref-1961nkrumah_270-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-1961nkrumah-270">[268]</a></sup> He mentions how Africa is a land of "vast riches" with mineral resources from that "range from gold and diamonds to uranium and petroleum".<sup id="cite_ref-1961nkrumah_270-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-1961nkrumah-270">[268]</a></sup> Nkrumah says that the reason Africa is not thriving right now is because the European powers have been taking all the wealth for themselves. If Africa could be independent of European rule, he said, then it could truly flourish and contribute positively to the world. In the ending words of this speech Nkrumah calls his people to action by saying "This is our chance.<sup id="cite_ref-271"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-271">[269]</a></sup> We must act now. Tomorrow may be too late and the opportunity will have passed, and with it the hope of free Africa's survival".<sup id="cite_ref-1961nkrumah_270-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-1961nkrumah-270">[268]</a></sup> This rallied the nation in a nationalistic movement.<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>

In his honour, an annual event called "Journey to Nkroful" was set up to celebrate his birthday.<sup id="cite_ref-272"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-272">[270]</a></sup> Mausoleum and Museum at [Nkroful](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nkroful "Nkroful"), Western Region have been named after him that showcase some of the artifacts he used when alive.<sup id="cite_ref-273"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-273">[271]</a></sup>

Kwame Nkrumah Memorial Park & Museum, Accra has been there to keep memory of him. Also, University of Science and Technology was changed to Kwame Nkrumah University of Science and Technology to recognise his support building a strong education system in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-274"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-274">[272]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-275"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-275">[273]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-276"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-276">[274]</a></sup>

## Personal life\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Personal life")\]

Kwame Nkrumah married [Fathia Ritzk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fathia_Nkrumah "Fathia Nkrumah"), an [Egyptian Coptic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copts "Copts") bank worker and former teacher, on the evening of her arrival in Ghana: New Year's Eve, 1957–1958.<sup id="cite_ref-277"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-277">[275]</a></sup> Fathia's mother refused to bless their marriage, after another one of her children left with a foreign husband.<sup id="cite_ref-278"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-278">[276]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-279"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-279">[277]</a></sup>

As a married couple, the Nkrumah family had three children: [Gamal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamal_Nkrumah "Gamal Nkrumah") (born 1958), [Samia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samia_Nkrumah "Samia Nkrumah") (born 1960) and Sekou (born 1964). Gamal is a newspaper journalist, while Samia and Sekou are politicians. Nkrumah also has another son, Francis, a paediatrician (born 1935).<sup id="cite_ref-280"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-280">[278]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-281"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-281">[279]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Onsy_282-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-Onsy-282">[280]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-283"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-283">[281]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-284">[282]</a></sup>

## Cultural depictions\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Cultural depictions")\]

In the 2010 book _[The Other Wes Moore](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Other_Wes_Moore "The Other Wes Moore"),_ Nkrumah, during his time in the United States, is noted to have served as a mentor to the author's grandfather for several months upon the [immigration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration "Immigration") of the author's family into the country.<sup id="cite_ref-285"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-285">[283]</a></sup>

Nkrumah is played by [Danny Sapani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Danny_Sapani "Danny Sapani") in the [Netflix](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netflix "Netflix") television series _[The Crown](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Crown_(TV_series) "The Crown (TV series)")_ (season 2, episode 8 "Dear Mrs Kennedy"). The show's portrayal of the historical significance of the Queen's visit to Ghana and dance with Nkrumah has been described as exaggerated in one source interviewing Nat Nuno-Amarteifio, later mayor of Accra, who was a teenage student at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-286"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-286">[284]</a></sup>

_African's Black Star: The Legacy of Kwame Nkrumah_ is a 2011 film about the rise and fall of this colonial rebellion leader.

A golden statue of Nkrumah is a centerpiece in Ghanaian rapper [Serious Klein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serious_Klein "Serious Klein")'s 2021 video "Straight Outta Pandemic".<sup id="cite_ref-287"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_note-287">[285]</a></sup>

> The essence of neo-colonialism is that the State which is subject to it is, in theory, independent and has all the outward trappings of international sovereignty. In reality its economic system and thus its political policy is directed from outside.
> 
> — Introduction

## Notes\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kwame_Nkrumah&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Notes")\]

1.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_ref-33 "Jump up")** Nyanibah survived her son, watching over him throughout his life. For a time after his death, she guarded his tomb. See [Birmingham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#CITEREFBirmingham), p. 3.
2.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_ref-82 "Jump up")** Members swore an oath of secrecy, pledging to "irrevocably obey" orders from the group, to "help a member brother of THE CIRCLE in all things and in all difficulties", to avoid the use of violence, to fast on the twenty-first day of the month, and finally, to "accept the leadership of Kwame Nkrumah". See: "[The Circle](https://books.google.com/books?id=h6wsBgAAQBAJ&pg=PA389)" in _Nationalism in Asia and Africa_ by Elie Kedourie, 1970.

35.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_ref-36 "Jump up")** ["Fathia Nkrumah: Farewell to all that"](https://web.archive.org/web/20170821212904/http://weekly.ahram.org.eg/Archive/2000/499/profile.htm). _Al-Ahram Weekly_. 20 September 2009. Archived from [the original](http://weekly.ahram.org.eg/Archive/2000/499/profile.htm) on 21 August 2017. Retrieved 21 September 2017. Grandmother Nyaneba, then well into her 90s, waited patiently for her son. Mother stood by her side. Grandmother was determined to remain alive to witness Nkrumah's triumphant return to Ghana. Only after her hand was placed on his coffin did the old woman at last accept that he was dead. Grandmother was to pass away seven years later in my mother's arms, aged 102.

232.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_ref-Beynahm93_234-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah#cite_ref-Beynahm93_234-1) Baynham (1988), _Military and Politics in Nkrumah's Ghana_, p. 93. "Within a week, 1,193 Ghanaian soldiers were in Léopoldvile and 192 more were waiting for transport in Accra with 156 trucks and 160 tons of stores. In terms of its resources, Ghana made one of the heaviest manpower contributions to the Congo. By the end of August 1960, she had 2,394 army officers and men in the country. The Ghana contingent remained for three of the four years of the UN operations, contributing a total of more than 39,000-man-months."
